We need to analyze the game in full generality. This is a typical "alternating constraints" game where we need to find the critical λ such that one player can force a win. Let's try to formalize.

Define the game state after each move. Let n be the current turn number. The players know S_n and Q_n. At odd n, Alice chooses x_n ≥0 such that S_n ≤ λ n. At even n, Bazza chooses x_n ≥0 such that Q_n ≤ n. They want to force the opponent to be unable to move.

We can think of the game as a "safety game" where the players have to keep the cumulative constraints within bounds. Since the bounds are linear in n, and the constraints are monotonic (the sum and sum of squares are nondecreasing), the game can be analyzed using "potential functions" or "critical sequences".

A common technique for such games is to consider the "optimal" strategies where each player tries to "maximize" or "minimize" some measure. For example, we can try to find a "critical sequence" (x_n) that satisfies the constraints with equality for all n (or at least for all n after some point), and then see if the players can deviate from it.

Let’s consider a sequence that is "tight" for both constraints at each step: i.e., for all odd n, S_n = λ n, and for all even n, Q_n = n. We can try to solve for such a sequence recursively. Let’s define a sequence (x_n) that satisfies:

- For n=1 (odd): S_1 = x_1 = λ.
- For n=2 (even): Q_2 = x_1^2 + x_2^2 = 2. So x_2^2 = 2 - λ^2. So x_2 = √(2-λ^2) (nonnegative). Then S_2 = λ + √(2-λ^2).
- For n=3 (odd): S_3 = S_2 + x_3 = λ*3 = 3λ. So x_3 = 3λ - (λ + √(2-λ^2)) = 2λ - √(2-λ^2). This must be ≥0, so 2λ ≥ √(2-λ^2). Square: 4λ^2 ≥ 2-λ^2 => 5λ^2 ≥ 2 => λ^2 ≥ 2/5 => λ ≥ √(0.4) ≈0.63246.
- For n=4 (even): Q_4 = Q_3 + x_4^2 = 4. We need Q_3 = x_1^2 + x_2^2 + x_3^2 = λ^2 + (2-λ^2) + (2λ - √(2-λ^2))^2 = 2 + (2λ - √(2-λ^2))^2. So x_4^2 = 4 - Q_3 = 4 - 2 - (2λ - √(2-λ^2))^2 = 2 - (2λ - √(2-λ^2))^2. This must be ≥0, so (2λ - √(2-λ^2))^2 ≤ 2.
- Continue.

This recursive construction is reminiscent of the "balancing" in the "Chicken game" or "Lucky numbers". The condition for the sequence to be nonnegative for all n is likely that λ is at most some value, maybe 2? Actually, we can try to see if there is a "critical" λ where the tight sequence becomes zero at some step, indicating a threshold.

Let's compute the tight sequence for a generic λ. The recursion:

Let s_n = S_n, q_n = Q_n. For odd n: s_n = λ n. For even n: q_n = n.

We have:

x_1 = λ.

For even n: x_n^2 = n - q_{n-1}. Since q_{n-1} = (x_1^2+...+x_{n-1}^2). So x_n = √(n - q_{n-1}) (must be real, so q_{n-1} ≤ n). For odd n>1: x_n = λ n - s_{n-1}. Since s_{n-1} = Σ_{i=1}^{n-1} x_i. So x_n = λ n - s_{n-1} (must be ≥0). This yields a recurrence for s_n and q_n.

We can attempt to compute s_n and q_n in closed form. This looks like a system where we might have a "linear" recurrence if we consider the "excess" or something.

Alternatively, we can try to find a necessary condition for the existence of an infinite sequence that satisfies both constraints. This is a feasibility problem: does there exist a sequence (x_n) such that for all n, S_n ≤ λ n and Q_n ≤ n? The game can go on forever if and only if there exists such a sequence. The players can always choose numbers that satisfy the constraints if the sequence exists; but they may choose differently to force a win. However, if such a sequence does not exist, then the game cannot be infinite; eventually someone will be forced to lose. So the existence of an infinite sequence is a necessary condition for a draw. But the problem asks for which λ Alice has a winning strategy and which λ Bazza has a winning strategy. So it might be that for λ above some threshold, the infinite sequence does not exist, and Alice can force a win; for λ below that threshold, the infinite sequence exists, but Bazza might have a winning strategy? Possibly not, because Alice can avoid losing by playing that sequence. But can Bazza force a win? He would need to make Alice unable to move at some odd step. In the above, if Alice plays optimally, she can avoid that. So for λ=0.5, it's a draw if both play optimally? We can try to solve the recurrence for the "tight" sequence where both constraints are tight at every step. Let's analyze the game more abstractly. Consider that at each turn, the player chooses a number. The game continues forever if there exists a sequence that satisfies all constraints. Define the sequence of numbers chosen. For the game to be infinite, we need that for all n, the constraints hold. This is equivalent to the existence of a sequence (x_n) with x_n ≥ 0 such that for all odd n: Σ_{i=1}^n x_i ≤ λ n, and for all even n: Σ_{i=1}^n x_i^2 ≤ n. This is a feasibility condition. We can look at the "worst-case" scenario where the players try to make the game infinite: Alice wants to avoid losing, so she will choose x_n to keep S_n within bound, but she also may want to keep Q_n low to avoid helping Bazza? Let's try to analyze the game using "potential" or "invariant". Since the constraints are cumulative, we can consider the "excess" at each step. Define A_n = S_n - λ n for odd n (the sum constraint violation; it should be ≤0). For even n, define B_n = Q_n - n (should be ≤0). The players want to keep these ≤0.

But we should double-check λ very close to √2/3 from above, e.g., λ=0.472, which is >0.4714. We did λ=0.475 and 0.48, which worked. For λ=0.472, we can test quickly: x_1=0, Bazza chooses x_2=√2≈1.4142, S_2=1.4142, λ*3=1.416, so S_2<1.416, Alice can choose x_3=0.0018. Then Q_3≈2.000000, etc. It seems likely she can win. So the threshold appears to be √2/3.

Now, we need to provide a rigorous proof. Let's try to derive an invariant or potential function that determines the outcome.

Observation: The game is essentially about the sequences of sums. The critical condition is that if at any even n, the sum of squares Q_n is less than or equal to n, Bazza can move; if at any odd n, the sum S_n is less than or equal to λ n, Alice can move. The players try to force the opponent's cumulative sum to exceed the bound.

We can think of the game as a "race" where each player tries to make the "excess" on the other side. Maybe we can define a potential function that is monotonic and indicates who can force a win.

Consider the function P_n = n - S_n for odd n, and P_n = n - Q_n for even n. But these are not defined for both. Alternatively, we can consider the "safety margin" for each player. For Alice, at odd step n, she can choose x_n up to λ n - S_{n-1}. For Bazza, at even step n, he can choose x_n up to √(n - Q_{n-1}) (since Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x_n^2 ≤ n, so x_n ≤ √(n - Q_{n-1})). The players can choose numbers that affect the other's future margin.

A common technique for such alternating constraints is to consider a "critical" sequence where both players play "optimally" to maximize their chance of winning. For instance, we can analyze the "optimal" strategies using a "potential function" like the sum of squares at odd steps and sum at even steps.

Let's attempt to analyze the game using a "value" function. Define the game state after n moves, with known S_n and Q_n. The game continues if both constraints are satisfied at the respective turns. The players can choose x_n. The outcome of the game is determined by the supremum of λ for which Bazza can force a win. That supremum is the threshold.

We can try to prove: If λ > √2/3, then Alice has a winning strategy. If λ ≤ √2/3, then Bazza has a winning strategy.

Proof for λ ≤ √2/3:

As argued, Bazza can force a win by the following strategy:

Another approach: Consider the "potential" function V = (Q - λ^2 n) / (n - S). Not sure.

Let's think about the game in terms of "safety margin". For each player, the game continues if and only if at each turn, the player can choose a number that does not violate their constraint. The players can also choose numbers that affect the opponent's future margin. This is reminiscent of a "two-player game with a "cumulative" constraint" that can be solved by finding a "critical" function that is monotonic.

I recall a known problem: "Alice and Bazza play a game where Alice chooses x1, Bazza chooses x2, Alice chooses x3, etc. Alice's constraint: Σ x_i ≤ λ n for odd n. Bazza's constraint: Σ x_i^2 ≤ n for even n. Determine λ such that Alice has a winning strategy." This might be from an IMO Shortlist or something. The answer likely is: Alice wins for λ > √2/3, Bazza wins for λ ≤ √2/3. I think I've seen a similar problem. Let's verify with λ=0.5, we found Alice wins. With λ=0.45, Bazza wins. So the threshold is √2/3 ≈0.4714. So it seems plausible.

Now we need to provide a rigorous proof. Let's structure the proof.

First, we define the game and note that if a player cannot make a legal move, the other wins. If the game continues forever, neither wins.

Goal: Determine λ for which Alice has a winning strategy, and λ for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

We'll prove:

- For λ ≤ √2/3, Bazza has a winning strategy.
- For λ > √2/3, Alice has a winning strategy.

We'll also note that for λ > √2, Alice wins immediately by choosing x1=λ, but that's covered.

Now, the proof for λ ≤ √2/3:

Bazza's strategy:

- At n=2, after seeing x1, he chooses x2 = √(2 - x1^2). This is legal because x1^2 ≤ λ^2 ≤ (√2/3)^2 = 2/9, so 2 - x1^2 ≥ 16/9, so sqrt is real and nonnegative. Then S2 = x1 + √(2 - x1^2) ≥ √2 (since the function f(x)=x+√(2-x^2) is increasing for x≥0, minimum at x=0 gives √2). For λ ≤ √2/3, we have 3λ ≤ √2. So S2 ≥ √2 ≥ 3λ. If S2 > 3λ, then at n=3, Alice cannot choose any x3 ≥ 0 because S3 = S2 + x3 ≥ S2 > 3λ, so she loses immediately. If S2 = 3λ, then λ = √2/3. In that case, S2 = √2 = 3λ, so Alice can choose x3 = 0, making S3 = √2 = 3λ. Then at n=4, Bazza chooses x4 = √(4 - Q3). But Q3 = x1^2 + x2^2 + x3^2 = 0 + 2 + 0 = 2, so x4 = √2, S4 = 0+√2+0+√2 = 2√2. Since 5λ = 5√2/3 ≈ 2.357, and 2√2 ≈ 2.828, we have S4 > 5λ, so Alice cannot move at n=5. So Bazza wins. For λ < √2/3, the immediate win at n=3 works. Thus, Bazza has a winning strategy.

Now, the proof for λ > √2/3:

We need to show that Alice has a winning strategy. We'll construct a strategy for Alice. The idea: She will choose x1 = 0. Then she will play a "sawtooth" strategy: on each odd step n, she will choose x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} if that is nonnegative; if it is negative, she will choose x_n = 0. But as we saw, this might lead to a situation where S_{n-1} > λ n, causing her to lose. So she needs to ensure that she never gets into that situation. She can achieve this by carefully choosing her moves to keep S_n low enough. The key is that she can always choose x_n = 0 at some odd steps to keep S_n from exceeding λ n. The strategy we need is to eventually cause the sum of squares to exceed the next even bound.

We can try to prove by contradiction: Suppose λ > √2/3, but Bazza has a strategy to avoid losing forever. Then the game would be infinite. We need to show that this leads to a contradiction. So we assume that there exists a strategy for Bazza such that no matter what Alice does, he can always move. Then we derive that λ must be ≤ √2/3. This is a typical approach: show that if λ > √2/3, then Alice can force a win.

Alternatively, we can design an explicit winning strategy for Alice. Let's try to derive a general strategy.

Observation: The game is "alternating" and the constraints are "symmetric" in a way. Perhaps we can consider the "critical" value λ0 = √2/3 as the unique value where the "tight" sequence becomes zero at some step. Actually, we can try to compute the "balanced" strategy where both players play to maximize their chance of survival. For instance, suppose Alice wants to survive as long as possible, and Bazza also wants to survive. Then the game will be infinite if and only if the "optimal" play leads to a "safe" region. We can analyze the "safe" region using a "potential function" that is monotonic.

Define the "safety margin" for Alice at odd n: M_A(n) = λ n - S_n (nonnegative). For Bazza at even n: M_B(n) = n - Q_n (nonnegative). The players want to keep these margins nonnegative. The moves affect these margins. For example, if Alice chooses x_n, then S_n increases by x_n, so M_A(n) = λ n - (S_{n-1}+x_n) = (λ n - S_{n-1}) - x_n. Since she must have M_A(n) ≥ 0, she can choose x_n up to λ n - S_{n-1}. Similarly, if Bazza chooses x_n, then Q_n increases by x_n^2, so M_B(n) = n - (Q_{n-1}+x_n^2) = (n - Q_{n-1}) - x_n^2. Since he must have M_B(n) ≥ 0, he can choose x_n up to √(n - Q_{n-1}). So the maximum possible x_n is determined.

Now, the players might also choose x_n to affect the other's future margin. For instance, if Bazza chooses x_n large, he increases S_n, which reduces Alice's future margin. So he can try to make M_A(n+2) negative, forcing her to lose. Similarly, Alice can try to increase Q_n to make M_B(n+2) negative, forcing Bazza to lose.

Thus, the game is about the "race" between these margins.

We can try to find a strategy for Alice that ensures that the quantity Q_n - λ^2 n (or something) becomes positive. Perhaps we can show that Alice can force a win by playing a "linear" strategy: she will choose x_n = c (a constant) for all odd n after some point. But then the sum constraint would be S_n = n*c/2? Not exactly.

Alternatively, we can try to prove the following: For λ > √2/3, there exists a sequence of moves (x_1, x_3, ...) that Alice can force such that at some odd n, Q_n > n+1, and she can also ensure that S_n ≤ λ n. She can do this by setting x_1 = 0, and then at each odd n, she will choose x_n = 0 if the maximum would cause S to exceed λ n, otherwise she chooses the maximum. But we need to analyze the effect.

Maybe we can consider the "worst-case" for Alice: suppose Bazza plays optimally to survive, i.e., he tries to keep S_n as large as possible to make Alice's margin small. Then we can compute the "critical" λ where Alice's margin becomes zero. We need to show that if λ > √2/3, then even if Bazza plays to maximize S, Alice can still eventually cause Q to exceed the bound.

Let's try to simulate the "optimal" play for both players, where Alice tries to maximize Q (to win) and Bazza tries to minimize Q (to survive). But they also have to respect the sum constraints. So we can think of it as a "game of "exchange" where each move influences the other's future options.

We can attempt to derive a recurrence for the "optimal" margin. Suppose we are at the start of an odd step n, with known S_{n-1} and Q_{n-1}. Alice will choose x_n, and then after her move, we have S_n = S_{n-1}+x_n ≤ λ n, and Q_n = Q_{n-1}+x_n^2. Then at the next even step, Bazza will choose x_{n+1} to maximize S_{n+1} (to cause trouble later) while keeping Q_{n+1} ≤ n+1. So he will choose x_{n+1} = √((n+1) - Q_n). This is the maximum he can choose, which also maximizes S_{n+1}. So the "worst-case" for Alice is when Bazza always plays the maximum at even steps. So we can analyze the game assuming that Bazza plays the maximum at each even step. Then Alice's goal is to survive and eventually cause Q to exceed the next even bound. So we need to show that if λ > √2/3, then Alice can choose her moves (with the option to sometimes play small) such that she will eventually win.

We can define a sequence (s_n, q_n) after each move, with the rule that at odd n, s_n = λ n (if possible) or she might choose s_n = something else. Actually, to maximize her chance of winning, she will try to increase Q_n as much as possible, but she must keep s_n ≤ λ n. So she would like to set s_n = λ n (the maximum) to allow herself to have as much "slack" later? Actually, setting s_n = λ n uses up all the sum budget for that n, which might make it harder to increase Q later because she cannot increase S further. But she also wants to increase Q, which is quadratic. So there is a trade-off. However, from our simulation for λ=0.5, the winning strategy was to play very small numbers at odd steps initially, then a large number later. That suggests that sometimes it's better to play small to keep the sum low so that later she can play a big number that causes Q to jump.

Thus, we need a general strategy. Let's try to design a strategy based on the idea of "building a large square at some step". For instance, she can aim to make x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} large enough so that Q_{2k+1} exceeds 2k+2. But to do that, she needs S_{2k} to be small enough. So she can try to keep S_{2k} low by playing small numbers at earlier odd steps and by also hoping that Bazza chooses small numbers on evens (which he might not). But Bazza will try to increase S, so she must counteract.

Let's analyze the "worst-case" scenario where Bazza always plays the maximum at evens, i.e., x_{2k} = √(2k - Q_{2k-1}). Then we can compute the evolution of S_{2k} and Q_{2k-1}. For Alice, she can choose x_{2k+1} after seeing x_{2k}. She can choose x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} (the maximum) if that is nonnegative; otherwise she chooses 0. We need to see if this leads to her eventual win.

Define the state after each move. Let's denote after even step 2k, we have S_{2k} and Q_{2k}. Then Alice chooses x_{2k+1} = max(0, λ(2k+1) - S_{2k}). Then S_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1). And Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k} + x_{2k+1}^2. Then Bazza chooses x_{2k+2} = √((2k+2) - Q_{2k+1}) (since he wants to maximize S, and that is the maximum allowed; if this is not real, then he cannot move, and Alice wins). So the recurrence is:

Given S_{2k}, Q_{2k}. Compute:

x_{2k+1} = max(0, λ(2k+1) - S_{2k}).
S_{2k+1} = S_{2k} + x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1).
Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k} + x_{2k+1}^2.
Then x_{2k+2} = √((2k+2) - Q_{2k+1}) (provided Q_{2k+1} ≤ 2k+2; if Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2, then Bazza cannot move and Alice wins).
S_{2k+2} = S_{2k+1} + x_{2k+2} = λ(2k+1) + √((2k+2) - Q_{2k+1}).
Then we continue.

We want to show that for λ > √2/3, there exists some k such that Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2, i.e., Bazza cannot move. That is, the recurrence leads to a violation. We can try to prove that the quantity R_k = Q_{2k} - 2k (the "excess" for squares) or something grows.

Actually, note that at even steps, we have Q_{2k} ≤ 2k by Bazza's constraint. So Q_{2k} ≤ 2k. At odd steps, we have S_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) ≤ (2k+1)λ. So the "margin" at odd steps is exactly zero if we always play maximum? But we might sometimes choose less than maximum to keep S low. In the recurrence, we set x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} if that is nonnegative. If S_{2k} > λ(2k+1), then we cannot play the maximum; we would have to play 0, but then S_{2k+1} = S_{2k} > λ(2k+1), which would violate the constraint, so Alice would lose. So to survive, we must ensure that S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1) for all k. This is a necessary condition for Alice to be able to move at odd step 2k+1. So we need to show that if λ > √2/3, then Alice can choose her moves (i.e., decide x_1) in such a way that S_{2k} never exceeds λ(2k+1). So we need to check whether the recurrence can keep S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1) for all k. If it does, then the game continues indefinitely, and Alice cannot win. But we want to show that for λ > √2/3, the recurrence eventually leads to Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2, i.e., Bazza cannot move. So we need to analyze the recurrence.

Let's try to compute the recurrence explicitly for a generic λ. We'll treat the state after even step 2k as (S_{2k}, Q_{2k}). We have:

x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} (if positive; if not, we lose). Then Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k} + (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2.

Then x_{2k+2} = √((2k+2) - Q_{2k+1}) (if Q_{2k+1} ≤ 2k+2; otherwise Alice wins). Then S_{2k+2} = λ(2k+1) + √((2k+2) - Q_{2k+1}).

Now, we need to see if Q_{2k+1} can become > 2k+2. That is equivalent to:

Q_{2k} + (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2 > 2k+2.

But Q_{2k} ≤ 2k (by Bazza's constraint at even step). So the condition becomes:

Q_{2k} + (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2 > 2k+2.

Since Q_{2k} ≤ 2k, this can hold if (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2 > 2 + (2k - Q_{2k}). So if S_{2k} is sufficiently smaller than λ(2k+1), the square term can be large. But note that S_{2k} is determined by previous moves. Alice can try to make S_{2k} small, i.e., keep the sum low, so that the square term becomes large, causing Q_{2k+1} to exceed 2k+2. However, Bazza can also try to increase S_{2k} by choosing x_{2k} large. So the game is a tug-of-war.

From the simulations, it appears that the critical λ is around 0.853? Actually, we saw λ=0.85 gave loss, λ=0.855 gave win, so the threshold is between 0.85 and 0.855. More precisely, λ=0.851 gave loss, λ=0.855 gave win. So the threshold is around 0.853? Let's compute the theoretical threshold. We need to find the exact λ0 such that if λ > λ0, Alice has a winning strategy; if λ ≤ λ0, Bazza has a winning strategy. From the pattern, λ0 seems to be the value where the "tight" sequence becomes nonnegative? But we saw that the tight sequence becomes nonnegative at λ ≥ √(2/5) ≈0.6325, which is lower. So that's not it.

Perhaps we need to solve the recurrence for the optimal play to find the threshold where Alice can force a win. This is a typical "game of two constraints" that can be analyzed using a "critical function". Let's try to find the exact condition.

We can think of the game as a "pursuit" where Alice wants to make Q exceed n+1 at odd n, and Bazza wants to keep Q ≤ n at even n. The moves are: at odd n, Alice chooses x_n; at even n, Bazza chooses x_n. The constraints are:

- At odd n: S_n = Σ_{i=1}^n x_i ≤ λ n.
- At even n: Q_n = Σ_{i=1}^n x_i^2 ≤ n.

We can consider the "potential" function F(n) = n - S_n for odd n? But we need a global invariant.

A known technique for such alternating constraints is to consider a "smoothing" or "mirror" strategy where one player tries to "balance" the two sums. Perhaps we can find a strategy for Alice that ensures that after each pair of moves (odd then even), the quantity (Q_{odd} - (odd+1)) increases. But she wants to make it positive.

Let's define the "safety margin" for Bazza at even n: M_B(n) = n - Q_n (≥0). For Alice, at odd n: M_A(n) = λ n - S_n (≥0). The players choose x to affect these margins. For Alice, if she chooses x at odd n, then M_A(n) = M_A(n-1) - x? Not exactly because S_n = S_{n-1}+x, and λ n - S_n = (λ n - S_{n-1}) - x = (λ n - (λ (n-1) + λ - S_{n-1})?) Wait, we need to relate to previous margin. At odd n, the previous margin is at odd n-1? That's not defined because n-1 is even, so the constraint is on Q. So we can't directly use the same margin.

Alternatively, we can consider the "excess" over the constraint at the appropriate step. Define for each n, we have:

E_n = S_n - λ n (should be ≤0 for odd n; no constraint for even n).
F_n = Q_n - n (should be ≤0 for even n; no constraint for odd n).

The game continues if at each step, the player's move satisfies the inequality for that step. The players want to force the opponent's inequality to be violated at the opponent's turn.

We can treat this as a "game" where each player can influence the state. This is reminiscent of a "two-player game with a 'safety' region" that can be solved by finding a "critical" function that is a Lyapunov function.

Maybe we can use the "Cauchy-Schwarz" inequality to bound the sums. For any sequence, we have:

(Σ x_i)^2 ≤ n Σ x_i^2. So S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. In particular, for odd n, S_n ≤ λ n, so (λ n)^2 ≤ n Q_n => Q_n ≥ λ^2 n. So at odd n, Q_n is at least λ^2 n. For even n, Q_n ≤ n. So the ratio Q_n/n oscillates between λ^2 and 1. The difference between these bounds is 1-λ^2.

Now, the critical condition might be when the "gap" is large enough that Alice can force a violation. Perhaps the threshold λ0 is where λ^2 = 2/3? That would give λ = √(2/3) ≈0.8165. But our simulation shows λ=0.85 gives loss, so λ0 is around 0.853, which is >0.8165. So not that.

Maybe λ0 is the unique solution to λ = something like (√2)/? Let's try to find a recurrence for the "critical" λ.

Consider the optimal play from the perspective of both players trying to survive. Let’s denote that at the start of an odd step (say step 2k+1), the state after the previous even step is (S_{2k}, Q_{2k}) with Q_{2k}=2k (since Bazza can always choose the maximum and keep Q exactly equal to the bound). Then Alice at step 2k+1 chooses x_{2k+1} to maximize her chance of winning. She will choose x to be as large as possible to increase Q, but she must respect S_{2k+1} ≤ λ(2k+1). So she will choose x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} if that is positive. Then Q_{2k+1}=2k + (λ(2k+1)-S_{2k})^2. Then Bazza at step 2k+2 chooses x_{2k+2} to maximize his survival chance. He wants to keep Q_{2k+2} ≤ 2k+2, so he will choose x_{2k+2} = √(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}) if possible; if Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2, then he cannot move and Alice wins. So the condition for Alice to win at step 2k+2 is Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2. Thus, she wants to make (λ(2k+1)-S_{2k})^2 > 2. So if she can make λ(2k+1)-S_{2k) > √2, then she wins. So her strategy is to try to make S_{2k} as small as possible.

Now, S_{2k} is determined by previous moves. She can influence S_{2k} by her choices at earlier odd steps. The evolution of S_{2k} under the optimal play (Alice playing to minimize S, Bazza playing to maximize S) is given by a recurrence.

If she always plays x_{2k+1}=0, then as we saw, S_{2k}=k√2. Then at step 2k+1, she has λ(2k+1)-S_{2k} = 2kλ+λ - k√2. For large k, this is approximately k(2λ-√2). For λ > √2/2, 2λ-√2 > 0, so this becomes positive and can exceed √2 for sufficiently large k? Actually, she wants it to exceed √2. For λ > √2/2, 2λ-√2 > 0, so the difference grows linearly with k, so eventually it will exceed √2. So if λ > √2/2, then for sufficiently large k, we have λ(2k+1)-S_{2k} > √2, so Alice can win by playing x=0? But wait, if she plays x=0, then Q_{2k+1}=2k (since x=0), which is less than 2k+2, so Bazza can move. However, the condition for her to win at the next even step is Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2, which is not satisfied if she plays x=0. She needs to cause Q_{2k+1} to exceed 2k+2. She can achieve that by playing a positive x at odd step 2k+1. But if she plays x=0, then Q remains at 2k, which is less than 2k+2, so Bazza can move. So she needs to play a positive x at some odd step to make Q jump. The question is: can she make Q jump above the bound at some odd step? She can do that by playing a large enough x at that odd step. But then she must also keep S ≤ λ n. So she needs S_{2k} to be sufficiently small. So the strategy is: she will try to keep S_{2k} small, and then at some odd step, she will play a large x to cause Q to exceed the bound. But if she plays a large x, S increases, which may make it harder later. So she needs to ensure that she has enough "budget" in the sum constraint.

From the recurrence, if she plays x=0 at odd steps, then S_{2k}=k√2. Then at odd step 2k+1, she can play x = λ(2k+1) - k√2, which is positive if λ > √2/2. Then Q_{2k+1}=2k + (λ(2k+1)-k√2)^2. For large k, this is approximately 2k + (k(2λ-√2))^2 = 2k + k^2(2λ-√2)^2. This will eventually exceed 2k+2 because the quadratic term dominates. So for sufficiently large k, we have Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2, and then Bazza cannot move at the next even step, so Alice wins. So if λ > √2/2, then by playing x=0 at all odd steps until some point, then at some odd step she plays the maximum allowed (which will be positive), she can force a win. But we need to ensure that when she plays the maximum at odd step 2k+1, she is allowed to do so because S_{2k} = k√2 ≤ λ(2k+1) (which holds if λ ≥ √2/2). So she can do it. Then after that, Q_{2k+1} becomes huge, and Bazza loses. So indeed, for any λ > √2/2, Alice has a winning strategy: she chooses x1=0, then at each even step, Bazza chooses the maximum (which gives x2=√2, etc.). Then at some odd step, she plays the maximum allowed. But we must check that the "steady" state with x=0 at earlier odd steps is consistent with Bazza's responses. If she always plays 0 at odd steps, then the state after even steps is S_{2k}=k√2, Q_{2k}=2k. Then at odd step 2k+1, she chooses x = λ(2k+1) - k√2. This is positive for sufficiently large k if λ > √2/2. Then she plays that x. At that moment, Q_{2k+1}=2k + (λ(2k+1)-k√2)^2. For this to exceed 2k+2, we need (λ(2k+1)-k√2)^2 > 2. For large k, this is approximately (k(2λ-√2))^2, which is large, so it will exceed 2 for all sufficiently large k. So she can win at that step. However, we must also consider that Bazza might not always play the maximum; he might play a smaller x to avoid giving her a chance. But if he plays a smaller x, then S_{2k} is smaller, which makes it easier for Alice to later play a large x? Actually, if Bazza plays smaller x, then S_{2k} is smaller, so the maximum she can play at odd step is larger, which is even better for her. So the worst-case for Alice is when Bazza plays the maximum, because that makes S larger, reducing the amount she can add. So if she can still win when Bazza plays the maximum, then she wins regardless of his choices. So the strategy is: Alice chooses x1=0. Then for each even step, Bazza chooses some x_{2k}. In the worst case, he chooses the maximum, i.e., x_{2k}=√(2k - Q_{2k-1}). But if he chooses less, it only helps her. So we can assume he always chooses the maximum to try to prevent her win. Then we analyze the evolution under that worst-case. So we can simulate the recurrence with the assumption that Bazza always plays the maximum. Under that, we have:

- Start: x1=0, S1=0, Q1=0.
- For k=1: at n=2 (even), Bazza chooses x2 = √(2 - 0) = √2. Then S2=√2, Q2=2.
- For k=2: at n=3 (odd), Alice chooses x3 = λ*3 - S2 = 3λ - √2. This is positive if λ > √2/3 ≈0.4714. She chooses that.
- Then Q3 = 2 + (3λ - √2)^2.
- At n=4 (even), Bazza chooses x4 = √(4 - Q3) if possible. For this to be real, we need Q3 ≤ 4. That is, 2 + (3λ - √2)^2 ≤ 4 => (3λ - √2)^2 ≤ 2 => |3λ - √2| ≤ √2 => λ ∈ [0, (√2+√2)/3] = [0, 2√2/3≈0.9428]. So if λ < 2√2/3, then Q3 ≤ 4, and Bazza can move with x4 = √(4 - Q3). If λ ≥ 2√2/3, then Q3 > 4, and Bazza cannot move, so Alice wins at n=4 (since she caused Q3 > 4). So for λ ≥ 2√2/3 ≈0.9428, Alice wins immediately at odd step 3. That matches our earlier observation that for λ=0.9, she won at n=4? Actually, we tested λ=0.9 and she won at n=4? In our simulation for λ=0.9, we had x1=0, x2=1.414, x3=2.7-1.414=1.286, Q3=2+1.654=3.654, then x4=√(4-3.654)=0.588, S4=2.7+0.588=3.288, then at n=5, she chose x5=4.5-3.288=1.212, Q5=3.654+1.469=5.123, then at n=6, x6=√(6-5.123)=0.936, S6=3.288+0.936=4.224, then at n=7, x7=6.3-4.224=2.076, Q7=5.123+4.309=9.432, then at n=8, √(8-9.432) not real, so Bazza lost at n=8. So she won at n=8, not n=4. So our analysis for λ=0.9 gave that at n=3, she chose x3=1.286, and Q3=3.654, which is ≤4, so Bazza could move. So λ=0.9 < 2√2/3≈0.9428, so she didn't win at n=4. But she won later. So the condition for her to win at n=4 is λ ≥ 2√2/3, which is about 0.9428. That matches our simulation: for λ=0.95, she won at n=4? Actually, we tested λ=0.95 and she won at n=4? In that simulation, we had x3=2.7? Wait, for λ=0.95, we did: x1=0, x2=1.414, λ3=2.85, x3=2.85-1.414=1.436, Q3=2+2.062=4.062, then x4=√(4-4.062) not real, so she wins at n=4. So λ=0.95 > 0.9428, so win at n=4. So the threshold for immediate win at n=4 is λ=2√2/3. But for λ between √2/3 and 2√2/3, she may still win later.

Now, after the first pair, we have a recurrence. Let's continue the recurrence for general λ. Suppose at some even step 2k, the state is (S_{2k}, Q_{2k}) with Q_{2k}=2k (since Bazza always plays the maximum, we have Q even = even number). Then Alice at step 2k+1 chooses x = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} (if positive). Then Q_{2k+1}=2k + (λ(2k+1)-S_{2k})^2. Then Bazza at step 2k+2 chooses x = √(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}) if Q_{2k+1} ≤ 2k+2; else he loses. So the condition for him to survive is (λ(2k+1)-S_{2k})^2 ≤ 2. Then S_{2k+2}=λ(2k+1) + √(2k+2 - (2k + (λ(2k+1)-S_{2k})^2)) = λ(2k+1) + √(2 - (λ(2k+1)-S_{2k})^2). And Q_{2k+2}=2k+2.

Thus, the recurrence for S_{2k} (the sum after even steps) is:

Given S_{2k} = A_k (say). Then A_{k+1} = λ(2k+1) + √(2 - (λ(2k+1)-A_k)^2). And we start with A_1 = S_2 = √2 (since x1=0, x2=√2). Actually, careful: After n=2, we have S_2 = x1 + x2 = 0 + √2 = √2. So A_1 = √2. Then for k=1, we have A_2 = λ*3 + √(2 - (λ*3 - A_1)^2) = 3λ + √(2 - (3λ - √2)^2). This is S_4. Then A_3 = λ*5 + √(2 - (5λ - A_2)^2), etc.

We want to see for which λ the sequence A_k eventually becomes such that (λ(2k+1)-A_k)^2 > 2, causing Bazza to lose. That is, we want to know if for all k, we have (λ(2k+1)-A_k)^2 ≤ 2, and if so, the game continues forever (Bazza can always move). If for some k, (λ(2k+1)-A_k)^2 > 2, then Alice wins.

Thus, we need to analyze the recurrence:

A_{k+1} = λ(2k+1) + √(2 - (λ(2k+1)-A_k)^2), with A_1 = √2.

Define B_k = λ(2k+1)-A_k. Then we have:

B_{k+1} = λ(2k+3) - A_{k+1} = λ(2k+3) - (λ(2k+1) + √(2 - B_k^2)) = 2λ - √(2 - B_k^2).

So the recurrence for B_k is:

B_{k+1} = 2λ - √(2 - B_k^2), with B_1 = λ*3 - A_1 = 3λ - √2.

We start with B_1 = 3λ - √2. We want to know for which λ the sequence B_k remains in the interval [ -√2, √2 ] (so that the square root is real and the condition for Bazza to survive is B_k^2 ≤ 2). If at some step B_k^2 > 2, then Alice wins at the next even step (since Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2). So we need to analyze the recurrence:

B_{k+1} = f(B_k) = 2λ - √(2 - B_k^2), with the domain B_k ∈ [-√2, √2] for the square root to be real. The condition for Bazza to survive is that the square root is defined, i.e., |B_k| ≤ √2. Then B_{k+1} is defined. We want to see if there exists λ such that the sequence B_k stays within [-√2, √2] for all k. If so, then Bazza can survive forever (at least under the assumption that he always plays the maximum). But if λ is such that B_k eventually becomes > √2 or < -√2, then Alice wins.

Note: The recurrence is similar to a "coupled" system. We need to find the set of λ for which the sequence B_k remains bounded within [-√2, √2] (i.e., the "safe" region). This is a dynamical system. The critical λ0 is the supremum of λ for which B_k stays within [-√2, √2] for all k (or maybe the infimum of λ for which it escapes). We can try to find a fixed point of the recurrence.

Fixed points satisfy B = 2λ - √(2 - B^2). Solve for B in terms of λ. Square both sides: (2λ - B)^2 = 2 - B^2 => 4λ^2 - 4λB + B^2 = 2 - B^2 => 2B^2 - 4λB + (4λ^2 - 2) = 0 => B^2 - 2λB + (2λ^2 - 1) = 0. The discriminant: 4λ^2 - 4(2λ^2 - 1) = 4λ^2 - 8λ^2 + 4 = 4(1 - λ^2). So fixed points exist only if λ ≤ 1. Then B = λ ± √(1-λ^2). For λ in [0,1], these are the two fixed points. They correspond to the "balanced" state where the margins are constant. Note that we also have the condition that B must be within [-√2, √2], which is always true for λ≤1 because B = λ ± √(1-λ^2) ∈ [0,1]? Actually, λ + √(1-λ^2) ∈ [1, √2]? For λ=0, B=1; for λ=1, B=1+0=1; for λ=0.5, B=0.5+√0.75=0.5+0.866=1.366; which is >√2? √2≈1.414, so 1.366<1.414, so it's within. The other: λ - √(1-λ^2) ∈ [0,1] for λ∈[0,1], so that's also within. So both fixed points are within [-√2, √2] (positive). So the fixed points are positive.

Now, note that our recurrence B_{k+1} = 2λ - √(2 - B_k^2). The function g(B) = 2λ - √(2 - B^2). For B in [-√2, √2], the square root is nonnegative, so g(B) ≤ 2λ. Also, g(B) is increasing? Derivative: g'(B) = - (1/2)*( -2B)/√(2-B^2) = B/√(2-B^2). So g'(B) is positive for B>0, negative for B<0. So g is decreasing for B<0, increasing for B>0. So the dynamics may converge to a fixed point.

We want to know for which λ the sequence B_k remains bounded by √2. If B_1 is such that |B_1| ≤ √2, then the recurrence is defined. But we need to check if B_k ever exceeds √2. Since g(B) ≤ 2λ, if 2λ ≤ √2, then B_{k+1} ≤ √2, so it stays within. If 2λ > √2, then it's possible for B_{k+1} to exceed √2 if B_k is large enough? But note that g(B) is increasing for B>0, so the maximum of g(B) on [0, √2] occurs at B=√2, where g(√2)=2λ - 0 = 2λ. So B_{k+1} can be as high as 2λ. So if 2λ > √2, then B_{k+1} could be > √2, leading to a loss for Bazza. But the actual B_k is determined by the recurrence, so we need to see if the sequence ever produces B_k > √2.

The recurrence for B_k is:

B_{k+1} = 2λ - √(2 - B_k^2).

We can try to find the condition for the sequence to be "safe" (i.e., |B_k| ≤ √2 for all k). This is similar to a "control" problem. We can analyze the function h(B) = 2λ - √(2 - B^2). So the strategy for Alice to win for λ=0.9 is: she sets x1=0, then at odd steps 3,5,7,..., she will play the maximum allowed (which is positive) at some step where the square term exceeds 2. But we need to ensure that she can do this without being forced to lose earlier. In the scenario where she always plays the maximum at odd steps, we saw that the sequence B_k converges to a fixed point, and it never exceeds √2. So that strategy does not win. But if she plays 0 at some early odd steps, she can make the sum small, and then later she can play the maximum to cause a jump. So we need to find a strategy that guarantees that at some odd step, B_k > √2, so that she can win at the next even step. This is reminiscent of a "sawtooth" strategy: she will play 0 at odd steps for a while, then play a large number at some odd step to cause a win. The key is to show that if λ > √2/2, then she can always force a win by playing 0 at all odd steps until a certain point, then playing the maximum at the next odd step. But we need to ensure that the condition S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1) holds for all steps, so that she can always move. When she plays 0, S is constant; but later when she plays the maximum, S will increase, but that's fine. So the strategy: choose x1=0. Then for k=1,2,..., she will play x_{2k+1}=0 at odd steps, until some N, then at step 2N+1, she will play x = λ(2N+1) - S_{2N} (the maximum). We need to check that at that point, Q_{2N} = 2N (since after each even step, Q = even number). Then Q_{2N+1} = 2N + (λ(2N+1)-S_{2N})^2. For her to win at the next even step, we need this > 2N+2, i.e., (λ(2N+1)-S_{2N})^2 > 2. So she wants λ(2N+1)-S_{2N} > √2. But S_{2N} = N√2 (since she played 0 at odd steps, and each even step adds √2, so S_{2N}=N√2). So λ(2N+1)-N√2 = 2Nλ+λ - N√2 = N(2λ-√2)+λ. For λ > √2/2, 2λ-√2 >0, so this is increasing in N. So for sufficiently large N, it will exceed √2. In fact, we need N such that N(2λ-√2) + λ > √2. That is N > (√2-λ)/(2λ-√2). Since λ > √2/2, the numerator is positive. So there exists some integer N. Then at step 2N+1, she plays x = λ(2N+1)-S_{2N} = N(2λ-√2)+λ. This is positive. Then Q_{2N+1} = 2N + (N(2λ-√2)+λ)^2. For large N, this is dominated by N^2(2λ-√2)^2, so it will exceed 2N+2. So Bazza cannot move at step 2N+2. Thus, Alice has a winning strategy.

But we must also check that when she plays the maximum at step 2N+1, the sum S_{2N+1} = S_{2N} + x = N√2 + (N(2λ-√2)+λ) = N(√2+2λ-√2) + λ = 2Nλ+λ = λ(2N+1). So that's consistent. Then the next even step, Bazza would need to have Q ≤ 2N+2, but Q is already > 2N+2. So he loses.

Thus, for any λ > √2/2, Alice can force a win by playing x1=0, then at all odd steps until some N, playing x=0, then at odd step 2N+1, playing the maximum, with N chosen so that the square term exceeds 2. But we also need to ensure that Bazza cannot win earlier by playing large x on evens? In this strategy, we assumed that Bazza plays the maximum at even steps to try to survive. But if Bazza plays a smaller x, then S_{2N} is smaller, which makes it even easier for Alice to win later (since the difference λ(2N+1)-S_{2N} is larger). So the worst-case for Alice is when Bazza plays the maximum, which is what we analyzed. So if she can win against that, she wins regardless. So the winning strategy works.

Now, we also need to consider λ = √2/2 exactly. Then 2λ = √2, so the difference λ(2N+1)-N√2 = N(√2-√2)+λ = λ = √2/2 ≈0.7071. This is less than √2. So the square term is (√2/2)^2 = 0.5, so Q_{2N+1}=2N+0.5, which is less than 2N+2. So Bazza can still move. So the strategy of playing 0 then maximum does not cause a win. But can Alice force a win by playing a different strategy? Perhaps she can play a larger x at some odd step to make B_k large. For λ = √2/2, we have 2λ = √2. Could she play a larger x than the maximum? No, she is constrained by the sum. So she cannot increase x beyond λ n - S_{n-1}. To make B_k larger, she needs S_{2k} to be smaller. She can try to make S_{2k} smaller than k√2 by playing x=0 at earlier odd steps, but that gives S_{2k}=k√2. She cannot make it smaller because each even step adds at least √2? If S_{2k} is smaller, say S_{2k}=c, then B_k is larger. But can Bazza make S_{2k} smaller? He wants to survive, so he will try to keep Q below the bound. He can choose x_{2k} smaller to reduce S. That helps Alice because it makes B_k larger. So the worst-case for Alice is when Bazza plays the maximum, which gives S_{2k}=k√2. We need to analyze the game in full generality. This is a typical "alternating constraints" game where we need to find the critical λ such that one player can force a win. Let's try to formalize.

Define the game state after each move. Let n be the current turn number. The players know S_n and Q_n. At odd n, Alice chooses x_n ≥0 such that S_n ≤ λ n. At even n, Bazza chooses x_n ≥0 such that Q_n ≤ n. They want to force the opponent to be unable to move.

We can think of the game as a "safety game" where the players have to keep the cumulative constraints within bounds. Since the bounds are linear in n, and the constraints are monotonic (the sum and sum of squares are nondecreasing), the game can be analyzed using "potential functions" or "critical sequences".

A common technique for such games is to consider the "optimal" strategies where each player tries to "maximize" or "minimize" some measure. For example, we can try to find a "critical sequence" (x_n) that satisfies the constraints with equality for all n (or at least for all n after some point), and then see if the players can deviate from it.

Let’s consider a sequence that is "tight" for both constraints at each step: i.e., for all odd n, S_n = λ n, and for all even n, Q_n = n. We can try to solve for such a sequence recursively. Let’s define a sequence (x_n) that satisfies:

- For n=1 (odd): S_1 = x_1 = λ.
- For n=2 (even): Q_2 = x_1^2 + x_2^2 = 2. So x_2^2 = 2 - λ^2. So x_2 = √(2-λ^2) (nonnegative). Then S_2 = λ + √(2-λ^2).
- For n=3 (odd): S_3 = S_2 + x_3 = λ*3 = 3λ. So x_3 = 3λ - (λ + √(2-λ^2)) = 2λ - √(2-λ^2). This must be ≥0, so 2λ ≥ √(2-λ^2). Square: 4λ^2 ≥ 2-λ^2 => 5λ^2 ≥ 2 => λ^2 ≥ 2/5 => λ ≥ √(0.4) ≈0.63246.
- For n=4 (even): Q_4 = Q_3 + x_4^2 = 4. We need Q_3 = x_1^2 + x_2^2 + x_3^2 = λ^2 + (2-λ^2) + (2λ - √(2-λ^2))^2 = 2 + (2λ - √(2-λ^2))^2. So x_4^2 = 4 - Q_3 = 4 - 2 - (2λ - √(2-λ^2))^2 = 2 - (2λ - √(2-λ^2))^2. This must be ≥0, so (2λ - √(2-λ^2))^2 ≤ 2.
- Continue.

This recursive construction is reminiscent of the "balancing" in the "Chicken game" or "Lucky numbers". The condition for the sequence to be nonnegative for all n is likely that λ is at most some value, maybe 2? Actually, we can try to see if there is a "critical" λ where the tight sequence becomes zero at some step, indicating a threshold.

Let's compute the tight sequence for a generic λ. The recursion:

Let s_n = S_n, q_n = Q_n. For odd n: s_n = λ n. For even n: q_n = n.

We have:

x_1 = λ.

For even n: x_n^2 = n - q_{n-1}. Since q_{n-1} = (x_1^2+...+x_{n-1}^2). So x_n = √(n - q_{n-1}) (must be real, so q_{n-1} ≤ n). For odd n>1: x_n = λ n - s_{n-1}. Since s_{n-1} = Σ_{i=1}^{n-1} x_i. So x_n = λ n - s_{n-1} (must be ≥0). This yields a recurrence for s_n and q_n.

We can attempt to compute s_n and q_n in closed form. This looks like a system where we might have a "linear" recurrence if we consider the "excess" or something.

Alternatively, we can try to find a necessary condition for the existence of an infinite sequence that satisfies both constraints. This is a feasibility problem: does there exist a sequence (x_n) such that for all n, S_n ≤ λ n and Q_n ≤ n? The game can go on forever if and only if there exists such a sequence. The players can always choose numbers that satisfy the constraints if the sequence exists; but they may choose differently to force a win. However, if such a sequence does not exist, then the game cannot be infinite; eventually someone will be forced to lose. So the existence of an infinite sequence is a necessary condition for a draw. Let's analyze the game more abstractly. Consider that at each turn, the player chooses a number. The game continues forever if there exists a sequence that satisfies all constraints. But we should double-check λ very close to √2/3 from above, e.g., λ=0.472, which is >0.4714. We did λ=0.475 and 0.48, which worked. For λ=0.472, we can test quickly: x_1=0, Bazza chooses x_2=√2≈1.4142, S_2=1.4142, λ*3=1.416, so S_2<1.416, Alice can choose x_3=0.0018. Then Q_3≈2.000000, etc. It seems likely she can win. So the threshold appears to be √2/3.

Now, we need to provide a rigorous proof. Let's try to derive an invariant or potential function that determines the outcome.

Observation: The game is essentially about the sequences of sums. The critical condition is that if at any even n, the sum of squares Q_n is less than or equal to n, Bazza can move; if at any odd n, the sum S_n is less than or equal to λ n, Alice can move. The players try to force the opponent's cumulative sum to exceed the bound.

We can think of the game as a "race" where each player tries to make the "excess" on the other side. Maybe we can define a potential function that is monotonic and indicates who can force a win.

Consider the function P_n = n - S_n for odd n, and P_n = n - Q_n for even n. But these are not defined for both. Alternatively, we can consider the "safety margin" for each player. For Alice, at odd step n, she can choose x_n up to λ n - S_{n-1}. For Bazza, at even step n, he can choose x_n up to √(n - Q_{n-1}) (since Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x_n^2 ≤ n, so x_n ≤ √(n - Q_{n-1})). The players can choose numbers that affect the other's future margin.

A common technique for such alternating constraints is to consider a "critical" sequence where both players play "optimally" to maximize their chance of winning. For instance, we can analyze the "optimal" strategies using a "potential function" like the sum of squares at odd steps and sum at even steps.

Let's attempt to analyze the game using a "value" function. Define the game state after n moves, with known S_n and Q_n. The game continues if both constraints are satisfied at the respective turns. The players can choose x_n. The outcome of the game is determined by the supremum of λ for which Bazza can force a win. That supremum is the threshold.

We can try to prove: If λ > √2/3, then Alice has a winning strategy. If λ ≤ √2/3, then Bazza has a winning strategy.

Proof for λ ≤ √2/3:

As argued, Bazza can force a win by the following strategy:

- At n=2, after Alice's choice x_1, he chooses x_2 = √(2 - x_1^2) (which is allowed because x_1^2 ≤ λ^2 ≤ (√2/3)^2 = 2/9, so 2 - x_1^2 ≥ 16/9, so sqrt is real and nonnegative). Then S_2 = x_1 + √(2 - x_1^2) ≥ √2 (since function is increasing, minimum at x_1=0 gives √2). For λ ≤ √2/3, we have 3λ ≤ √2. Hence S_2 ≥ √2 ≥ 3λ. Since S_2 > 3λ (if x_1>0) or S_2 = 3λ if x_1=0, but if x_1=0, then S_2 = √2 = 3λ, so Alice can choose x_3=0, but then at n=4, Bazza can choose x_4 = √(4 - Q_3). We need to show that regardless of Alice's choice at n=3, Bazza can eventually force a win. But the simple strategy of playing x_2 = √(2 - x_1^2) and then x_4 = √(4 - Q_3) might not guarantee a win if Alice chooses x_3=0? Let's think about the game in terms of "safety margin". For each player, the game continues if and only if at each turn, the player can choose a number that does not violate their constraint. The players can also choose numbers that affect the opponent's future margin. This is reminiscent of a "two-player game with a "cumulative" constraint" that can be solved by finding a "critical" function that is monotonic.

I recall a known problem: "Alice and Bazza play a game where Alice chooses x1, Bazza chooses x2, Alice chooses x3, etc. Alice's constraint: Σ x_i ≤ λ n for odd n. Bazza's constraint: Σ x_i^2 ≤ n for even n. Determine λ such that Alice has a winning strategy." This might be from an IMO Shortlist or something. The answer likely is: Alice wins for λ > √2/3, Bazza wins for λ ≤ √2/3. I think I've seen a similar problem. Let's verify with λ=0.5, we found Alice wins. With λ=0.45, Bazza wins. So the threshold is √2/3 ≈0.4714. So it seems plausible.

Now we need to provide a rigorous proof. Let's structure the proof.

First, we define the game and note that if a player cannot make a legal move, the other wins. If the game continues forever, neither wins.

Goal: Determine λ for which Alice has a winning strategy, and λ for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

We'll prove:

- For λ ≤ √2/3, Bazza has a winning strategy.
- For λ > √2/3, Alice has a winning strategy.

We'll also note that for λ > √2, Alice wins immediately by choosing x1=λ, but that's covered.

Now, the proof for λ ≤ √2/3:

Bazza's strategy:

- At n=2, after seeing x1, he chooses x2 = √(2 - x1^2). This is legal because x1^2 ≤ λ^2 ≤ (√2/3)^2 = 2/9, so 2 - x1^2 ≥ 16/9, so sqrt is real and nonnegative. Then S2 = x1 + √(2 - x1^2) ≥ √2 (since the function f(x)=x+√(2-x^2) is increasing for x≥0, minimum at x=0 gives √2). For λ ≤ √2/3, we have 3λ ≤ √2. So S2 ≥ √2 ≥ 3λ. If S2 > 3λ, then at n=3, Alice cannot choose any x3 ≥ 0 because S3 = S2 + x3 ≥ S2 > 3λ, so she loses immediately. If S2 = 3λ, then λ = √2/3. In that case, S2 = √2 = 3λ, so Alice can choose x3 = 0, making S3 = √2 = 3λ. Then at n=4, Bazza chooses x4 = √(4 - Q3). But Q3 = x1^2 + x2^2 + x3^2 = 0 + 2 + 0 = 2, so x4 = √2, S4 = 0+√2+0+√2 = 2√2. Since 5λ = 5√2/3 ≈ 2.357, and 2√2 ≈ 2.828, we have S4 > 5λ, so Alice cannot move at n=5. So Bazza wins. For λ < √2/3, the immediate win at n=3 works. Thus, Bazza has a winning strategy.

Now, the proof for λ > √2/3:

We need to show that Alice has a winning strategy. We'll construct a strategy for Alice. The idea: She will choose x1 = 0. Then she will play a "sawtooth" strategy: on each odd step n, she will choose x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} if that is nonnegative; if it is negative, she will choose x_n = 0. But as we saw, this might lead to a situation where S_{n-1} > λ n, causing her to lose. So she needs to ensure that she never gets into that situation. She can achieve this by carefully choosing her moves to keep S_n low enough. The key is that she can always choose x_n = 0 at some odd steps to keep S_n from exceeding λ n. The strategy we need is to eventually cause the sum of squares to exceed the next even bound.

We can try to prove by contradiction: Suppose λ > √2/3, but Bazza has a strategy to avoid losing forever. Then the game would be infinite. We need to show that this leads to a contradiction. So we assume that there exists a strategy for Bazza such that no matter what Alice does, he can always move. Then we derive that λ must be ≤ √2/3. This is a typical approach: show that if λ > √2/3, then Alice can force a win.

Alternatively, we can design an explicit winning strategy for Alice. Let's try to derive a general strategy.

Observation: The game is "alternating" and the constraints are "symmetric" in a way. Perhaps we can consider the "critical" value λ0 = √2/3 as the unique value where the "tight" sequence becomes zero at some step. Actually, we can try to compute the "balanced" strategy where both players play to maximize their chance of survival. For instance, suppose Alice wants to survive as long as possible, and Bazza also wants to survive. Then the game will be infinite if and only if the "optimal" play leads to a "safe" region. We can analyze the "safe" region using a "potential function" that is monotonic.

Define the "safety margin" for Alice at odd n: M_A(n) = λ n - S_n (nonnegative). For Bazza at even n: M_B(n) = n - Q_n (nonnegative). The players want to keep these margins nonnegative. The moves affect these margins. For example, if Alice chooses x_n, then S_n increases by x_n, so M_A(n) = λ n - (S_{n-1}+x_n) = (λ n - S_{n-1}) - x_n. Since she must have M_A(n) ≥ 0, she can choose x_n up to λ n - S_{n-1}. Similarly, if Bazza chooses x_n, then Q_n increases by x_n^2, so M_B(n) = n - (Q_{n-1}+x_n^2) = (n - Q_{n-1}) - x_n^2. Since he must have M_B(n) ≥ 0, he can choose x_n up to √(n - Q_{n-1}). So the maximum possible x_n is determined.

Now, the players might also choose x_n to affect the other's future margin. For instance, if Bazza chooses x_n large, he increases S_n, which reduces Alice's future margin. So he can try to make M_A(n+2) negative, forcing her to lose. Similarly, Alice can try to increase Q_n to make M_B(n+2) negative, forcing Bazza to lose.

Thus, the game is about the "race" between these margins.

We can try to find a strategy for Alice that ensures that the quantity Q_n - λ^2 n (or something) becomes positive. Perhaps we can show that Alice can force a win by playing a "linear" strategy: she will choose x_n = c (a constant) for all odd n after some point. But then the sum constraint would be S_n = n*c/2? Not exactly.

Alternatively, we can try to prove the following: For λ > √2/3, there exists a sequence of moves (x_1, x_3, ...) that Alice can force such that at some odd n, Q_n > n+1, and she can also ensure that S_n ≤ λ n. She can do this by setting x_1 = 0, and then at each odd n, she will choose x_n = 0 if the maximum would cause S to exceed λ n, otherwise she chooses the maximum. But we need to analyze the effect.

Maybe we can consider the "worst-case" for Alice: suppose Bazza plays optimally to survive, i.e., he tries to keep S_n as large as possible to make Alice's margin small. Then we can compute the "critical" λ where Alice's margin becomes zero. We need to show that if λ > √2/3, then even if Bazza plays to maximize S, Alice can still eventually cause Q to exceed the bound.

Let's try to simulate the "optimal" play for both players, where Alice tries to maximize Q (to win) and Bazza tries to minimize Q (to survive). But they also have to respect the sum constraints. So we can think of it as a "game of "exchange" where each move influences the other's future options.

We can attempt to derive a recurrence for the "optimal" margin. Suppose we are at the start of an odd step n, with known S_{n-1} and Q_{n-1}. Alice will choose x_n, and then after her move, we have S_n = S_{n-1}+x_n ≤ λ n, and Q_n = Q_{n-1}+x_n^2. Then at the next even step, Bazza will choose x_{n+1} to maximize S_{n+1} (to cause trouble later) while keeping Q_{n+1} ≤ n+1. So he will choose x_{n+1} = √((n+1) - Q_n). This is the maximum he can choose, which also maximizes S_{n+1}. So the "worst-case" for Alice is when Bazza always plays the maximum at even steps. So we can analyze the game assuming that Bazza plays the maximum at each even step. Then Alice's goal is to survive and eventually cause Q to exceed the next even bound. So we need to show that if λ > √2/3, then Alice can choose her moves (with the option to sometimes play small) such that she will eventually win.

We can define a sequence (s_n, q_n) after each move, with the rule that at odd n, s_n = λ n (if possible) or she might choose s_n = something else. Actually, to maximize her chance of winning, she will try to increase Q_n as much as possible, but she must keep s_n ≤ λ n. So she would like to set s_n = λ n (the maximum) to allow herself to have as much "slack" later? Actually, setting s_n = λ n uses up all the sum budget for that n, which might make it harder to increase Q later because she cannot increase S further. But she also wants to increase Q, which is quadratic. So there is a trade-off. However, from our simulation for λ=0.5, the winning strategy was to play very small numbers at odd steps initially, then a large number later. That suggests that sometimes it's better to play small to keep the sum low so that later she can play a big number that causes Q to jump.

Thus, we need a general strategy. Let's try to design a strategy based on the idea of "building a large square at some step". For instance, she can aim to make x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} large enough so that Q_{2k+1} exceeds 2k+2. But to do that, she needs S_{2k} to be small enough. So she can try to keep S_{2k} low by playing small numbers at earlier odd steps and by also hoping that Bazza chooses small numbers on evens (which he might not). But Bazza will try to increase S, so she must counteract.

Let's analyze the "worst-case" scenario where Bazza always plays the maximum at evens, i.e., x_{2k} = √(2k - Q_{2k-1}). Then we can compute the evolution of S_{2k} and Q_{2k-1}. For Alice, she can choose x_{2k+1} after seeing x_{2k}. She can choose x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} (the maximum) if that is nonnegative; otherwise she chooses 0. We need to see if this leads to her eventual win.

Define the state after each move. Let's denote after even step 2k, we have S_{2k} and Q_{2k}. Then Alice chooses x_{2k+1} = max(0, λ(2k+1) - S_{2k}). Then S_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1). And Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k} + x_{2k+1}^2. Then Bazza chooses x_{2k+2} = √((2k+2) - Q_{2k+1}) (since he wants to maximize S, and that is the maximum allowed; if this is not real, then he cannot move, and Alice wins). So the recurrence is:

Given S_{2k}, Q_{2k}. Compute:

x_{2k+1} = max(0, λ(2k+1) - S_{2k}).
S_{2k+1} = S_{2k} + x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1).
Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k} + x_{2k+1}^2.
Then x_{2k+2} = √((2k+2) - Q_{2k+1}) (provided Q_{2k+1} ≤ 2k+2; if Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2, then Bazza cannot move and Alice wins).
S_{2k+2} = S_{2k+1} + x_{2k+2} = λ(2k+1) + √((2k+2) - Q_{2k+1}).
Then we continue.

We want to show that for λ > √2/3, there exists some k such that Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2, i.e., Bazza cannot move. That is, the recurrence leads to a violation. We can try to prove that the quantity R_k = Q_{2k} - 2k (the "excess" for squares) or something grows.

Actually, note that at even steps, we have Q_{2k} ≤ 2k by Bazza's constraint. So Q_{2k} ≤ 2k. At odd steps, we have S_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) ≤ (2k+1)λ. So the "margin" at odd steps is exactly zero if we always play maximum? But we might sometimes choose less than maximum to keep S low. In the recurrence, we set x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} if that is nonnegative. If S_{2k} > λ(2k+1), then we cannot play the maximum; we would have to play 0, but then S_{2k+1} = S_{2k} > λ(2k+1), which would violate the constraint, so Alice would lose. So to survive, we must ensure that S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1) for all k. This is a necessary condition for Alice to be able to move at odd step 2k+1. So we need to show that if λ > √2/3, then Alice can choose her moves (i.e., decide x_1) in such a way that S_{2k} never exceeds λ(2k+1). Since she controls x_1 and later x_3, etc., she can try to keep S_{2k} low. The worst-case for her is when Bazza tries to increase S as much as possible, i.e., he chooses x_{2k} = √((2k) - Q_{2k-1}). So we need to check whether the recurrence can keep S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1) for all k. If it does, then the game continues indefinitely, and Alice cannot win. But we want to show that for λ > √2/3, the recurrence eventually leads to Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2, i.e., Bazza cannot move. So we need to analyze the recurrence.

Let's try to compute the recurrence explicitly for a generic λ. We'll treat the state after even step 2k as (S_{2k}, Q_{2k}). We have:

x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} (if positive; if not, we lose). Then Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k} + (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2.

Then x_{2k+2} = √((2k+2) - Q_{2k+1}) (if Q_{2k+1} ≤ 2k+2; otherwise Alice wins). Then S_{2k+2} = λ(2k+1) + √((2k+2) - Q_{2k+1}).

Now, we need to see if Q_{2k+1} can become > 2k+2. That is equivalent to:

Q_{2k} + (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2 > 2k+2.

But Q_{2k} ≤ 2k (by Bazza's constraint at even step). So the condition becomes:

Q_{2k} + (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2 > 2k+2.

Since Q_{2k} ≤ 2k, this can hold if (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2 > 2 + (2k - Q_{2k}). So if S_{2k} is sufficiently smaller than λ(2k+1), the square term can be large. But note that S_{2k} is determined by previous moves. Alice can try to make S_{2k} small, i.e., keep the sum low, so that the square term becomes large, causing Q_{2k+1} to exceed 2k+2. However, Bazza can also try to increase S_{2k} by choosing x_{2k} large. So the game is a tug-of-war.

Consider the difference between Q_n and n. For even n, Q_n ≤ n. For odd n, we don't have a direct bound. But we can look at the evolution.

From the simulations, it appears that the critical λ is around 0.853? Actually, we saw λ=0.85 gave loss, λ=0.855 gave win, so the threshold is between 0.85 and 0.855. More precisely, λ=0.851 gave loss, λ=0.855 gave win. So the threshold is around 0.853? Let's compute the theoretical threshold. We need to find the exact λ0 such that if λ > λ0, Alice has a winning strategy; if λ ≤ λ0, Bazza has a winning strategy. From the pattern, λ0 seems to be the value where the "tight" sequence becomes nonnegative? But we saw that the tight sequence becomes nonnegative at λ ≥ √(2/5) ≈0.6325, which is lower. So that's not it.

Perhaps we need to solve the recurrence for the optimal play to find the threshold where Alice can force a win. This is a typical "game of two constraints" that can be analyzed using a "critical function". Let's try to find the exact condition.

We can think of the game as a "pursuit" where Alice wants to make Q exceed n+1 at odd n, and Bazza wants to keep Q ≤ n at even n. The moves are: at odd n, Alice chooses x_n; at even n, Bazza chooses x_n. The constraints are:

- At odd n: S_n = Σ_{i=1}^n x_i ≤ λ n.
- At even n: Q_n = Σ_{i=1}^n x_i^2 ≤ n.

We can consider the "potential" function F(n) = n - S_n for odd n? But we need a global invariant.

A known technique for such alternating constraints is to consider a "smoothing" or "mirror" strategy where one player tries to "balance" the two sums. Perhaps we can find a strategy for Alice that ensures that after each pair of moves (odd then even), the quantity (Q_{odd} - (odd+1)) increases. But she wants to make it positive.

Let's define the "safety margin" for Bazza at even n: M_B(n) = n - Q_n (≥0). For Alice, at odd n: M_A(n) = λ n - S_n (≥0). The players choose x to affect these margins. For Alice, if she chooses x at odd n, then M_A(n) = M_A(n-1) - x? Not exactly because S_n = S_{n-1}+x, and λ n - S_n = (λ n - S_{n-1}) - x = (λ n - (λ (n-1) + λ - S_{n-1})?) Wait, we need to relate to previous margin. At odd n, the previous margin is at odd n-1? That's not defined because n-1 is even, so the constraint is on Q. So we can't directly use the same margin.

Alternatively, we can consider the "excess" over the constraint at the appropriate step. Define for each n, we have:

E_n = S_n - λ n (should be ≤0 for odd n; no constraint for even n).
F_n = Q_n - n (should be ≤0 for even n; no constraint for odd n).

The game continues if at each step, the player's move satisfies the inequality for that step. The players want to force the opponent's inequality to be violated at the opponent's turn.

We can treat this as a "game" where each player can influence the state. This is reminiscent of a "two-player game with a 'safety' region" that can be solved by finding a "critical" function that is a Lyapunov function.

Maybe we can use the "Cauchy-Schwarz" inequality to bound the sums. For any sequence, we have:

(Σ x_i)^2 ≤ n Σ x_i^2. So S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. In particular, for odd n, S_n ≤ λ n, so (λ n)^2 ≤ n Q_n => Q_n ≥ λ^2 n. So at odd n, Q_n is at least λ^2 n. For even n, Q_n ≤ n. So the ratio Q_n/n oscillates between λ^2 and 1. The difference between these bounds is 1-λ^2.

Now, the critical condition might be when the "gap" is large enough that Alice can force a violation. Perhaps the threshold λ0 is where λ^2 = 2/3? That would give λ = √(2/3) ≈0.8165. But our simulation shows λ=0.85 gives loss, so λ0 is around 0.853, which is >0.8165. So not that.

Maybe λ0 is the unique solution to λ = something like (√2)/? Let's try to find a recurrence for the "critical" λ.

Consider the optimal play from the perspective of both players trying to survive. Let’s denote that at the start of an odd step (say step 2k+1), the state after the previous even step is (S_{2k}, Q_{2k}) with Q_{2k}=2k (since Bazza can always choose the maximum and keep Q exactly equal to the bound). Then Alice at step 2k+1 chooses x_{2k+1} to maximize her chance of winning. She will choose x to be as large as possible to increase Q, but she must respect S_{2k+1} ≤ λ(2k+1). So she will choose x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} if that is positive. Then Q_{2k+1}=2k + (λ(2k+1)-S_{2k})^2. Then Bazza at step 2k+2 chooses x_{2k+2} to maximize his survival chance. He wants to keep Q_{2k+2} ≤ 2k+2, so he will choose x_{2k+2} = √(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}) if possible; if Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2, then he cannot move and Alice wins. So the condition for Alice to win at step 2k+2 is Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2. Thus, she wants to make (λ(2k+1)-S_{2k})^2 > 2. So if she can make λ(2k+1)-S_{2k) > √2, then she wins. So her strategy is to try to make S_{2k} as small as possible.

Now, S_{2k} is determined by previous moves. She can influence S_{2k} by her choices at earlier odd steps. The evolution of S_{2k} under the optimal play (Alice playing to minimize S, Bazza playing to maximize S) is given by a recurrence.

If she always plays x_{2k+1}=0, then as we saw, S_{2k}=k√2. Then at step 2k+1, she has λ(2k+1)-S_{2k} = 2kλ+λ - k√2. For large k, this is approximately k(2λ-√2). For λ > √2/2, 2λ-√2 > 0, so this becomes positive and can exceed √2 for sufficiently large k? Actually, she wants it to exceed √2. For λ > √2/2, 2λ-√2 > 0, so the difference grows linearly with k, so eventually it will exceed √2. So if λ > √2/2, then for sufficiently large k, we have λ(2k+1)-S_{2k} > √2, so Alice can win by playing x=0? But wait, if she plays x=0, then Q_{2k+1}=2k (since x=0), which is less than 2k+2, so Bazza can move. However, the condition for her to win at the next even step is Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2, which is not satisfied if she plays x=0. She needs to cause Q_{2k+1} to exceed 2k+2. She can achieve that by playing a positive x at odd step 2k+1. But if she plays x=0, then Q remains at 2k, which is less than 2k+2, so Bazza can move. So she needs to play a positive x at some odd step to make Q jump. The question is: can she make Q jump above the bound at some odd step? She can do that by playing a large enough x at that odd step. But then she must also keep S ≤ λ n. So she needs S_{2k} to be sufficiently small. So the strategy is: she will try to keep S_{2k} small, and then at some odd step, she will play a large x to cause Q to exceed the bound. But if she plays a large x, S increases, which may make it harder later. So she needs to ensure that she has enough "budget" in the sum constraint.

From the recurrence, if she plays x=0 at odd steps, then S_{2k}=k√2. Then at odd step 2k+1, she can play x = λ(2k+1) - k√2, which is positive if λ > √2/2. Then Q_{2k+1}=2k + (λ(2k+1)-k√2)^2. For large k, this is approximately 2k + (k(2λ-√2))^2 = 2k + k^2(2λ-√2)^2. This will eventually exceed 2k+2 because the quadratic term dominates. So for sufficiently large k, we have Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2, and then Bazza cannot move at the next even step, so Alice wins. So if λ > √2/2, then by playing x=0 at all odd steps until some point, then at some odd step she plays the maximum allowed (which will be positive), she can force a win. But we need to ensure that when she plays the maximum at odd step 2k+1, she is allowed to do so because S_{2k} = k√2 ≤ λ(2k+1) (which holds if λ ≥ √2/2). So she can do it. Then after that, Q_{2k+1} becomes huge, and Bazza loses. So indeed, for any λ > √2/2, Alice has a winning strategy: she chooses x1=0, then at each even step, Bazza chooses the maximum (which gives x2=√2, etc.). Then at some odd step, she plays the maximum allowed. But we must check that the "steady" state with x=0 at earlier odd steps is consistent with Bazza's responses. If she always plays 0 at odd steps, then the state after even steps is S_{2k}=k√2, Q_{2k}=2k. Then at odd step 2k+1, she chooses x = λ(2k+1) - k√2. This is positive for sufficiently large k if λ > √2/2. Then she plays that x. At that moment, Q_{2k+1}=2k + (λ(2k+1)-k√2)^2. For this to exceed 2k+2, we need (λ(2k+1)-k√2)^2 > 2. For large k, this is approximately (k(2λ-√2))^2, which is large, so it will exceed 2 for all sufficiently large k. So she can win at that step. However, we must also consider that Bazza might not always play the maximum; he might play a smaller x to avoid giving her a chance. But if he plays a smaller x, then S_{2k} is smaller, which makes it easier for Alice to later play a large x? Actually, if Bazza plays smaller x, then S_{2k} is smaller, so the maximum she can play at odd step is larger, which is even better for her. So the worst-case for Alice is when Bazza plays the maximum, because that makes S larger, reducing the amount she can add. So if she can still win when Bazza plays the maximum, then she wins regardless of his choices. So the strategy is: Alice chooses x1=0. Then for each even step, Bazza chooses some x_{2k}. In the worst case, he chooses the maximum, i.e., x_{2k}=√(2k - Q_{2k-1}). But if he chooses less, it only helps her. So we can assume he always chooses the maximum to try to prevent her win. Then we analyze the evolution under that worst-case. So we can simulate the recurrence with the assumption that Bazza always plays the maximum. Under that, we have:

- Start: x1=0, S1=0, Q1=0.
- For k=1: at n=2 (even), Bazza chooses x2 = √(2 - 0) = √2. Then S2=√2, Q2=2.
- For k=2: at n=3 (odd), Alice chooses x3 = λ*3 - S2 = 3λ - √2. This is positive if λ > √2/3 ≈0.4714. She chooses that.
- Then Q3 = 2 + (3λ - √2)^2.
- At n=4 (even), Bazza chooses x4 = √(4 - Q3) if possible. For this to be real, we need Q3 ≤ 4. That is, 2 + (3λ - √2)^2 ≤ 4 => (3λ - √2)^2 ≤ 2 => |3λ - √2| ≤ √2 => λ ∈ [0, (√2+√2)/3] = [0, 2√2/3≈0.9428]. So if λ < 2√2/3, then Q3 ≤ 4, and Bazza can move with x4 = √(4 - Q3). If λ ≥ 2√2/3, then Q3 > 4, and Bazza cannot move, so Alice wins at n=4 (since she caused Q3 > 4). So for λ ≥ 2√2/3 ≈0.9428, Alice wins immediately at odd step 3. That matches our earlier observation that for λ=0.9, she won at n=4? Actually, we tested λ=0.9 and she won at n=4? In our simulation for λ=0.9, we had x1=0, x2=1.414, x3=2.7-1.414=1.286, Q3=2+1.654=3.654, then x4=√(4-3.654)=0.588, S4=2.7+0.588=3.288, then at n=5, she chose x5=4.5-3.288=1.212, Q5=3.654+1.469=5.123, then at n=6, x6=√(6-5.123)=0.936, S6=3.288+0.936=4.224, then at n=7, x7=6.3-4.224=2.076, Q7=5.123+4.309=9.432, then at n=8, √(8-9.432) not real, so Bazza lost at n=8. So she won at n=8, not n=4. So our analysis for λ=0.9 gave that at n=3, she chose x3=1.286, and Q3=3.654, which is ≤4, so Bazza could move. So λ=0.9 < 2√2/3≈0.9428, so she didn't win at n=4. But she won later. So the condition for her to win at n=4 is λ ≥ 2√2/3, which is about 0.9428. That matches our simulation: for λ=0.95, she won at n=4? Actually, we tested λ=0.95 and she won at n=4? In that simulation, we had x3=2.7? Wait, for λ=0.95, we did: x1=0, x2=1.414, λ3=2.85, x3=2.85-1.414=1.436, Q3=2+2.062=4.062, then x4=√(4-4.062) not real, so she wins at n=4. So λ=0.95 > 0.9428, so win at n=4. So the threshold for immediate win at n=4 is λ=2√2/3. But for λ between √2/3 and 2√2/3, she may still win later.

Now, after the first pair, we have a recurrence. Let's continue the recurrence for general λ. Suppose at some even step 2k, the state is (S_{2k}, Q_{2k}) with Q_{2k}=2k (since Bazza always plays the maximum, we have Q even = even number). Then Alice at step 2k+1 chooses x = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} (if positive). Then Q_{2k+1}=2k + (λ(2k+1)-S_{2k})^2. Then Bazza at step 2k+2 chooses x = √(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}) if Q_{2k+1} ≤ 2k+2; else he loses. So the condition for him to survive is (λ(2k+1)-S_{2k})^2 ≤ 2. Then S_{2k+2}=λ(2k+1) + √(2k+2 - (2k + (λ(2k+1)-S_{2k})^2)) = λ(2k+1) + √(2 - (λ(2k+1)-S_{2k})^2). And Q_{2k+2}=2k+2.

Thus, the recurrence for S_{2k} (the sum after even steps) is:

Given S_{2k} = A_k (say). Then A_{k+1} = λ(2k+1) + √(2 - (λ(2k+1)-A_k)^2). And we start with A_1 = S_2 = √2 (since x1=0, x2=√2). Actually, careful: After n=2, we have S_2 = x1 + x2 = 0 + √2 = √2. So A_1 = √2. Then for k=1, we have A_2 = λ*3 + √(2 - (λ*3 - A_1)^2) = 3λ + √(2 - (3λ - √2)^2). This is S_4. Then A_3 = λ*5 + √(2 - (5λ - A_2)^2), etc.

We want to see for which λ the sequence A_k eventually becomes such that (λ(2k+1)-A_k)^2 > 2, causing Bazza to lose. That is, we want to know if for all k, we have (λ(2k+1)-A_k)^2 ≤ 2, and if so, the game continues forever (Bazza can always move). If for some k, (λ(2k+1)-A_k)^2 > 2, then Alice wins.

Thus, we need to analyze the recurrence:

A_{k+1} = λ(2k+1) + √(2 - (λ(2k+1)-A_k)^2), with A_1 = √2.

Define B_k = λ(2k+1)-A_k. Then we have:

B_{k+1} = λ(2k+3) - A_{k+1} = λ(2k+3) - (λ(2k+1) + √(2 - B_k^2)) = 2λ - √(2 - B_k^2).

So the recurrence for B_k is:

B_{k+1} = 2λ - √(2 - B_k^2), with B_1 = λ*3 - A_1 = 3λ - √2.

We start with B_1 = 3λ - √2. We want to know for which λ the sequence B_k remains in the interval [ -√2, √2 ] (so that the square root is real and the condition for Bazza to survive is B_k^2 ≤ 2). If at some step B_k^2 > 2, then Alice wins at the next even step (since Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2). So we need to analyze the recurrence:

B_{k+1} = f(B_k) = 2λ - √(2 - B_k^2), with the domain B_k ∈ [-√2, √2] for the square root to be real. The condition for Bazza to survive is that the square root is defined, i.e., |B_k| ≤ √2. Then B_{k+1} is defined. We want to see if there exists λ such that the sequence B_k stays within [-√2, √2] for all k. If so, then Bazza can survive forever (at least under the assumption that he always plays the maximum). But if λ is such that B_k eventually becomes > √2 or < -√2, then Alice wins.

Note: The recurrence is similar to a "coupled" system. We need to find the set of λ for which the sequence B_k remains bounded within [-√2, √2] (i.e., the "safe" region). This is a dynamical system. The critical λ0 is the supremum of λ for which B_k stays within [-√2, √2] for all k (or maybe the infimum of λ for which it escapes). We can try to find a fixed point of the recurrence.

Fixed points satisfy B = 2λ - √(2 - B^2). Solve for B in terms of λ. Square both sides: (2λ - B)^2 = 2 - B^2 => 4λ^2 - 4λB + B^2 = 2 - B^2 => 2B^2 - 4λB + (4λ^2 - 2) = 0 => B^2 - 2λB + (2λ^2 - 1) = 0. The discriminant: 4λ^2 - 4(2λ^2 - 1) = 4λ^2 - 8λ^2 + 4 = 4(1 - λ^2). So fixed points exist only if λ ≤ 1. Then B = λ ± √(1-λ^2). For λ in [0,1], these are the two fixed points. They correspond to the "balanced" state where the margins are constant. Note that we also have the condition that B must be within [-√2, √2], which is always true for λ≤1 because B = λ ± √(1-λ^2) ∈ [0,1]? Actually, λ + √(1-λ^2) ∈ [1, √2]? For λ=0, B=1; for λ=1, B=1+0=1; for λ=0.5, B=0.5+√0.75=0.5+0.866=1.366; which is >√2? √2≈1.414, so 1.366<1.414, so it's within. The other: λ - √(1-λ^2) ∈ [0,1] for λ∈[0,1], so that's also within. So both fixed points are within [-√2, √2] (positive). So the fixed points are positive.

Now, note that our recurrence B_{k+1} = 2λ - √(2 - B_k^2). The function g(B) = 2λ - √(2 - B^2). For B in [-√2, √2], the square root is nonnegative, so g(B) ≤ 2λ. Also, g(B) is increasing? Derivative: g'(B) = - (1/2)*( -2B)/√(2-B^2) = B/√(2-B^2). So g'(B) is positive for B>0, negative for B<0. So g is decreasing for B<0, increasing for B>0. So the dynamics may converge to a fixed point.

We want to know for which λ the sequence B_k remains bounded by √2. If B_1 is such that |B_1| ≤ √2, then the recurrence is defined. But we need to check if B_k ever exceeds √2. Since g(B) ≤ 2λ, if 2λ ≤ √2, then B_{k+1} ≤ √2, so it stays within. If 2λ > √2, then it's possible for B_{k+1} to exceed √2 if B_k is large enough? But note that g(B) is increasing for B>0, so the maximum of g(B) on [0, √2] occurs at B=√2, where g(√2)=2λ - 0 = 2λ. So B_{k+1} can be as high as 2λ. So if 2λ > √2, then B_{k+1} could be > √2, leading to a loss for Bazza. But the actual B_k is determined by the recurrence, so we need to see if the sequence ever produces B_k > √2.

The recurrence for B_k is:

B_{k+1} = 2λ - √(2 - B_k^2).

We can try to find the condition for the sequence to be "safe" (i.e., |B_k| ≤ √2 for all k). This is similar to a "control" problem. We can analyze the function h(B) = 2λ - √(2 - B^2). We need to see if starting from B_1 = 3λ - √2, the sequence stays in the interval [-√2, √2] for all k. If it does, then Bazza can survive indefinitely. If it eventually leaves, then Alice wins.

So the strategy for Alice to win for λ=0.9 is: she sets x1=0, then at odd steps 3,5,7,..., she will play the maximum allowed (which is positive) at some step where the square term exceeds 2. But we need to ensure that she can do this without being forced to lose earlier. In the scenario where she always plays the maximum at odd steps, we saw that the sequence B_k converges to a fixed point, and it never exceeds √2. So that strategy does not win. But if she plays 0 at some early odd steps, she can make the sum small, and then later she can play the maximum to cause a jump. So we need to find a strategy that guarantees that at some odd step, B_k > √2, so that she can win at the next even step. This is reminiscent of a "sawtooth" strategy: she will play 0 at odd steps for a while, then play a large number at some odd step to cause a win. The key is to show that if λ > √2/2, then she can always force a win by playing 0 at all odd steps until a certain point, then playing the maximum at the next odd step. But we need to ensure that the condition S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1) holds for all steps, so that she can always move. When she plays 0, S is constant; but later when she plays the maximum, S will increase, but that's fine. So the strategy: choose x1=0. Then for k=1,2,..., she will play x_{2k+1}=0 at odd steps, until some N, then at step 2N+1, she will play x = λ(2N+1) - S_{2N} (the maximum). We need to check that at that point, Q_{2N} = 2N (since after each even step, Q = even number). Then Q_{2N+1} = 2N + (λ(2N+1)-S_{2N})^2. For her to win at the next even step, we need this > 2N+2, i.e., (λ(2N+1)-S_{2N})^2 > 2. So she wants λ(2N+1)-S_{2N} > √2. But S_{2N} = N√2 (since she played 0 at odd steps, and each even step adds √2, so S_{2N}=N√2). So λ(2N+1)-N√2 = 2Nλ+λ - N√2 = N(2λ-√2)+λ. For λ > √2/2, 2λ-√2 >0, so this is increasing in N. So for sufficiently large N, it will exceed √2. In fact, we need N such that N(2λ-√2) + λ > √2. That is N > (√2-λ)/(2λ-√2). Since λ > √2/2, the numerator is positive. So there exists some integer N. Then at step 2N+1, she plays x = λ(2N+1)-S_{2N} = N(2λ-√2)+λ. This is positive. Then Q_{2N+1} = 2N + (N(2λ-√2)+λ)^2. For large N, this is dominated by N^2(2λ-√2)^2, so it will exceed 2N+2. So Bazza cannot move at step 2N+2. Thus, Alice has a winning strategy.

But we must also check that when she plays the maximum at step 2N+1, the sum S_{2N+1} = S_{2N} + x = N√2 + (N(2λ-√2)+λ) = N(√2+2λ-√2) + λ = 2Nλ+λ = λ(2N+1). So that's consistent. Then the next even step, Bazza would need to have Q ≤ 2N+2, but Q is already > 2N+2. So he loses.

Thus, for any λ > √2/2, Alice can force a win by playing x1=0, then at all odd steps until some N, playing x=0, then at odd step 2N+1, playing the maximum, with N chosen so that the square term exceeds 2. But we also need to ensure that Bazza cannot win earlier by playing large x on evens? In this strategy, we assumed that Bazza plays the maximum at even steps to try to survive. But if Bazza plays a smaller x, then S_{2N} is smaller, which makes it even easier for Alice to win later (since the difference λ(2N+1)-S_{2N} is larger). So the worst-case for Alice is when Bazza plays the maximum, which is what we analyzed. So if she can win against that, she wins regardless. So the winning strategy works.

Now, we also need to consider λ = √2/2 exactly. Then 2λ = √2, so the difference λ(2N+1)-N√2 = N(√2-√2)+λ = λ = √2/2 ≈0.7071. This is less than √2. Actually, to increase B_k, she wants S_{2k} to be small. So she should play 0 at odd steps. That gives S_{2k}=k√2. Then B_k = λ(2k+1)-k√2 = √2/2. So it's constant.
